ASSISTANT:
Here's the full picture, tying together the hash, the lookup, and the lock.

**1. The cache key — `compute_content_hash`** (`app/core/hashing.py:8-16`)
```python
def compute_content_hash(code: str) -> str:
    normalized = code.replace("\r\n", "\n").strip()
    payload = f"{settings.rules_version}\0{settings.ollama_model}\0{normalized}"
    return hashlib.sha256(payload.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest()
```
The hash isn't just over the code — it bundles in `RULES_VERSION` and `OLLAMA_MODEL`. So identical code gets a *different* hash if you change the model or bump the rules version, meaning old verdicts (produced under different rules/model) are never silently reused.

**2. The lookup — `find_reusable_scan`** (`app/db/repository.py`)
```python
REUSABLE_STATUSES = (PENDING, RUNNING, COMPLETED)  # NOT failed

async def find_reusable_scan(session, content_hash, ttl_hours=None):
    stmt = (
        select(Scan)
        .where(
            Scan.content_hash == content_hash,
            Scan.status.in_(REUSABLE_STATUSES),
            Scan.created_at >= _cutoff(ttl_hours),
        )
        .order_by(Scan.created_at.desc())
        .limit(1)
    )
    ...
```
`failed` is deliberately excluded — resubmitting after a failure always gets a fresh attempt, never reuses a dead result.

**3. Where the lock comes in — `submit_scan`** (`app/api/routes.py:69-91`)
```python
async with lock:
    await repo.fail_stale_scans(session)
    existing = await repo.find_reusable_scan(session, content_hash)
    if existing is not None:
        ...
        return ScanSubmitResponse(scan_id=existing.id, status=existing.status, cached=True)

    if not limiter.try_acquire():
        raise HTTPException(503, ...)

    try:
        scan = await repo.create_scan(session, content_hash, file.filename, code)
    except Exception:
        limiter.release()
        raise
# lock released here — task spawned outside the lock
task = asyncio.create_task(run_scan(scan.id, code, llm_client=client, release_slot=limiter.release))
```

`lock` is `cache_lock = asyncio.Lock()` — a single module-level singleton shared by **every** request (`app/api/routes.py:23`).

**Why it's needed — the race it prevents**

This is the classic "check then act" race. Imagine two requests upload the *exact same file* at almost the same instant, with no lock:

1. Request A: `find_reusable_scan` → nothing yet → proceeds.
2. Request B: `find_reusable_scan` → A hasn't committed yet, so B *also* sees nothing → proceeds.
3. Both A and B call `create_scan` → **two scan rows, two `run_scan` tasks, double the LLM calls** — for identical content.

The lock fixes this by making "check the cache, and if it's a miss, create the row" one atomic step from the perspective of concurrent requests:

1. A acquires `cache_lock` first. Finds nothing reusable → acquires a capacity slot → `create_scan` (commits a new `pending` row) → releases the lock.
2. B was blocked on `cache_lock` this whole time. Now it acquires the lock, runs `find_reusable_scan` again — and this time **A's row is there** (committed, status `pending`, within `REUSABLE_STATUSES`). B returns `cached: true, status: "pending", scan_id: <A's id>` — `202`, no new scan, no extra LLM calls.

This is exactly `tests/test_cache.py`'s concurrent-dedup test (`asyncio.gather` of two identical uploads → exactly one scan row, one set of LLM calls).

**Why this works with `asyncio.Lock` (single-threaded event loop)**

Only one coroutine can be *inside* the lock at a time, even though it `await`s things (like `repo.create_scan`'s `session.commit()`) while holding it. Any other coroutine calling `async with lock:` simply suspends until the first one exits the block — guaranteeing no interleaving between "look up" and "create" for two requests racing on the same content hash.

**Why the background task is spawned *outside* the lock**

`run_scan` can take seconds-to-minutes (LLM calls). If we held `cache_lock` for that whole duration, *every other request* — even ones for completely different files/hashes — would queue up behind it. The lock only needs to protect the brief "decide what to do" critical section; once `create_scan` has committed, the lock is released immediately and the (potentially slow) scan runs independently.

**Bonus: why `fail_stale_scans` runs first, inside the same lock**

If a `running` row for this hash had gotten orphaned (the bug we fixed earlier), `find_reusable_scan` would otherwise treat it as "in-flight" and hand back `cached: true` pointing at a dead scan. Running `fail_stale_scans` first (still under the lock) flips any such row to `failed` *before* the lookup, so it's excluded from `REUSABLE_STATUSES` and a fresh scan gets created instead.

USER:
got it!, the realese of the lock is happen automaticly at the end of the "with" block? and also can you create for me a flowchart using mermaid with short explanations of the system?

ASSISTANT:
**Lock auto-release**: yes. `async with lock:` is a context manager — `lock.release()` is called automatically by `__aexit__` when the block exits, **whether it exits normally or via an exception**. So even the `raise` inside the nested `except Exception: limiter.release(); raise` (for a `create_scan` failure) still happens *inside* the `async with lock:` block — as that exception propagates out, the lock is released as part of unwinding, before it reaches the caller. You never need to release it manually.

---

Here's the system flowchart:

```mermaid
flowchart TD
    subgraph SUBMIT["POST /scans"]
        A1["Validate upload<br/>.py, non-empty, size limit, UTF-8"] -->|invalid| A1E["422 Unprocessable"]
        A1 --> A2["compute_content_hash<br/>(rules_version + model + code)"]
        A2 --> A3["acquire cache_lock"]
        A3 --> A4["fail_stale_scans<br/>flip orphaned 'running' rows to 'failed'"]
        A4 --> A5{"find_reusable_scan<br/>same hash, not expired,<br/>status in pending/running/completed?"}
        A5 -->|found| A6["return cached=true<br/>200 if completed, else 202"]
        A5 -->|not found| A7{"CapacityLimiter.try_acquire()"}
        A7 -->|full| A8["503 Service Unavailable"]
        A7 -->|slot free| A9["create_scan<br/>insert row, status=pending"]
        A9 --> A10["release cache_lock"]
        A10 --> A11["spawn run_scan as background task"]
        A11 --> A12["return 202<br/>scan_id, pending, cached=false"]
    end

    subgraph RUNSCAN["run_scan (background task)"]
        B1["mark_running"] --> B2["for each rule"]
        B2 --> B3["build_prompt"]
        B3 --> B4["llm_client.complete<br/>asyncio.wait_for(timeout)"]
        B4 -->|ok| B5["parse_verdict"]
        B5 --> B6["save_rule_result"]
        B6 --> B2
        B2 -->|all rules done| B8["mark_completed"]
        B4 -->|timeout/error| B7["mark_failed(error)<br/>(itself wrapped in try/except)"]
        B8 --> B9["release capacity slot<br/>(finally)"]
        B7 --> B9
    end

    A11 -.spawns.-> B1

    subgraph GETSCAN["GET /scans/{id}"]
        C1["get_scan<br/>filter: created_at >= TTL cutoff"] -->|not found/expired| C2["404 Not Found"]
        C1 --> C3{"status == running AND<br/>older than SCAN_STALE_SECONDS?"}
        C3 -->|yes| C4["flip to failed<br/>error = 'Scan timed out'"]
        C3 -->|no| C5["return scan"]
        C4 --> C5
        C5 --> C6["status + per-rule results"]
    end

    subgraph CLEANUP["Periodic loop, every CLEANUP_INTERVAL_SECONDS"]
        D1["fail_stale_scans<br/>bulk: stale running -> failed"] --> D2["delete_expired_scans<br/>delete rows older than RESULT_TTL_HOURS"]
    end
```

**Quick legend:**

- **POST /scans** — validates the upload, then under `cache_lock`: sweeps stale rows, checks for a reusable scan (cache hit → return immediately, no LLM calls), otherwise checks capacity (`503` if full) and inserts a new `pending` row. The lock is released *before* spawning the background task, so slow scans never block other requests.
- **run_scan** — runs sequentially per rule (LLM call with a hard `asyncio.wait_for` timeout → parse → persist). Any failure (including timeout, or a failure inside `mark_failed` itself) ends in `mark_failed`/log + the capacity slot being released in `finally`.
- **GET /scans/{id}** — TTL-expired rows look like `404`. A `running` row that's been stuck longer than `SCAN_STALE_SECONDS` is lazily flipped to `failed` right when read.
- **Cleanup loop** — the same "stale running → failed" and "TTL → delete" logic, run periodically as a safety net for rows nobody is actively polling.

Want this added to the README as documentation?

ASSISTANT:
**1. Yes — one more place: `app/main.py:26`**

```python
@contextlib.asynccontextmanager
async def lifespan(app: FastAPI) -> AsyncIterator[None]:
    await init_db()
    task = asyncio.create_task(_cleanup_loop())   # <-- the periodic cleanup loop
    try:
        yield
    finally:
        task.cancel()
        with contextlib.suppress(asyncio.CancelledError):
            await task
```

That's the periodic `_cleanup_loop()` we discussed (TTL deletes + stale-running sweep) — it's also a background task, started once at app startup.

**2. Why `_background_tasks` is needed despite having `task`**

This comes down to **how long the local `task` variable stays alive**, and Python's asyncio docs have an explicit warning about this:

> *"Save a reference to the result of this function, to avoid a task disappearing mid-execution. The event loop only keeps weak references to tasks. A task that isn't referenced elsewhere may get garbage collected at any time, even before it's done."*

**In `routes.py`** (`submit_scan`):
```python
task = asyncio.create_task(run_scan(...))
_background_tasks.add(task)
task.add_done_callback(_background_tasks.discard)
...
return ScanSubmitResponse(...)   # function returns right away
```
`task` is a *local variable inside `submit_scan`*. As soon as `submit_scan` returns its response (which happens almost immediately — it doesn't wait for `run_scan`), that local variable goes out of scope. With nothing else holding a strong reference, the `Task` object becomes eligible for garbage collection — and since the event loop only holds a *weak* reference, the GC could collect it **mid-scan**, silently killing it with no error.

`_background_tasks` (a module-level `set`) holds a **strong reference** for the task's whole lifetime, preventing this. `task.add_done_callback(_background_tasks.discard)` then removes it from the set once it finishes — otherwise the set would grow forever as a memory leak.

**In `main.py`** (`lifespan`), no extra set is needed, because the situation is different:
```python
async def lifespan(app: FastAPI):
    await init_db()
    task = asyncio.create_task(_cleanup_loop())
    try:
        yield   # <-- suspended here for the entire app lifetime
    finally:
        task.cancel()
        await task
```
`lifespan` is an async generator that's **suspended at `yield` for as long as the app is running** — its local variable `task` stays alive in that suspended frame the whole time, so it already has a strong reference without needing a set. On shutdown, `lifespan` resumes and explicitly cancels/awaits it.

So the rule of thumb: if the coroutine that calls `create_task` returns quickly (like a request handler), you need something else (a set, in this case) to keep the task alive; if it stays suspended for the task's whole lifetime (like `lifespan`), the local variable itself is enough.

   - **`app/reviewer/scanner.py`** (MODIFIED this session) - current full state:
     ```python
     from __future__ import annotations

     import asyncio
     import logging
     from collections.abc import Callable

     from app.config import settings
     from app.db import repository as repo
     from app.db.session import async_session_factory
     from app.llm.client import LLMClient
     from app.llm.parsing import VERDICT_SCHEMA, parse_verdict
     from app.reviewer.rules import RULES

     logger = logging.getLogger(__name__)

     async def _run(fn, *args):
         async with async_session_factory() as session:
             return await fn(session, *args)

     async def run_scan(
         scan_id: str,
         code: str,
         *,
         llm_client: LLMClient,
         release_slot: Callable[[], None],
     ) -> None:
         """Run all configured rule checks for a scan and persist the results.

         Always ends with the scan marked completed or failed and the capacity
         slot released, even if an LLM call or response parse fails partway through.
         """
         try:
             await _run(repo.mark_running, scan_id)

             for rule in RULES:
                 prompt = rule.build_prompt(code)
                 try:
                     raw = await asyncio.wait_for(
                         llm_client.complete(prompt, json_schema=VERDICT_SCHEMA),
                         timeout=settings.llm_timeout_seconds,
                     )
                 except TimeoutError:
                     raise TimeoutError(
                         f"LLM call for rule '{rule.id}' exceeded {settings.llm_timeout_seconds}s"
                     ) from None
                 adheres = parse_verdict(raw)
                 await _run(repo.save_rule_result, scan_id, rule.id, rule.name, adheres, None)

             await _run(repo.mark_completed, scan_id)
         except Exception as exc:
             logger.exception("Scan %s failed", scan_id)
             try:
                 await _run(repo.mark_failed, scan_id, str(exc))
             except Exception:
                 logger.exception("Scan %s: also failed to record failure status", scan_id)
         finally:
             release_slot()
     ```
     Importance: hardened error handling (Part A of the user's request) - timeout enforcement + nested try/except around `mark_failed`.

     ```python
         ollama_base_url: str = "http://localhost:11434"
         ollama_model: str = "qwen2.5-coder:7b"
         llm_timeout_seconds: int = 120
         max_parallel_scans: int = 5
         result_ttl_hours: int = 24
         cleanup_interval_seconds: int = 600
         scan_stale_seconds: int = 300
         database_url: str = "sqlite+aiosqlite:///./code_review.db"
         rules_version: str = "v1"
         max_file_size_bytes: int = 1_000_000

     @lru_cache
     def get_settings() -> Settings:
         return Settings()

     settings = get_settings()
     ```

   - **`app/db/repository.py`** (MODIFIED) - full current relevant content:
     ```python
     from __future__ import annotations

     import datetime as dt

     from sqlalchemy import delete, select, update
     from sqlalchemy.ext.asyncio import AsyncSession
     from sqlalchemy.orm import selectinload

     from app.config import settings
     from app.db.models import RuleResult, Scan, ScanStatus, utcnow

     REUSABLE_STATUSES = (ScanStatus.PENDING.value, ScanStatus.RUNNING.value, ScanStatus.COMPLETED.value)

     def _cutoff(ttl_hours: int | None) -> dt.datetime:
         hours = settings.result_ttl_hours if ttl_hours is None else ttl_hours
         return utcnow() - dt.timedelta(hours=hours)

     def _stale_cutoff() -> dt.datetime:
         return utcnow() - dt.timedelta(seconds=settings.scan_stale_seconds)

     async def find_reusable_scan(...):  # unchanged
         ...

     async def create_scan(...):  # unchanged
         ...

     async def mark_running(...):  # unchanged
         ...

     async def save_rule_result(...):  # unchanged
         ...

     async def mark_completed(...):  # unchanged
         ...

     async def mark_failed(...):  # unchanged
         ...

     async def get_scan(session: AsyncSession, scan_id: str, ttl_hours: int | None = None) -> Scan | None:
         stmt = (
             select(Scan)
             .where(Scan.id == scan_id, Scan.created_at >= _cutoff(ttl_hours))
             .options(selectinload(Scan.results))
         )
         result = await session.execute(stmt)
         scan = result.scalar_one_or_none()
         if scan is not None and scan.status == ScanStatus.RUNNING.value and scan.created_at < _stale_cutoff():
             scan.status = ScanStatus.FAILED.value
             scan.completed_at = utcnow()
             scan.error = "Scan timed out"
             await session.commit()
         return scan

     async def fail_stale_scans(session: AsyncSession) -> int:
         stmt = (
             update(Scan)
             .where(Scan.status == ScanStatus.RUNNING.value, Scan.created_at < _stale_cutoff())
             .values(status=ScanStatus.FAILED.value, completed_at=utcnow(), error="Scan timed out")
         )
         result = await session.execute(stmt)
         await session.commit()
         return result.rowcount or 0

     async def delete_expired_scans(session: AsyncSession, ttl_hours: int | None = None) -> int:
         stmt = delete(Scan).where(Scan.created_at < _cutoff(ttl_hours))
         result = await session.execute(stmt)
         await session.commit()
         return result.rowcount or 0
     ```
     Importance: core of the stale-running watchdog - lazy fixup on `get_scan`, bulk sweep `fail_stale_scans`.

   - **`app/api/routes.py`** (MODIFIED, one line added) - current full content (lines 1-100):
     ```python
     from __future__ import annotations

     import asyncio

     from fastapi import APIRouter, Depends, File, HTTPException, Response, UploadFile, status
     from sqlalchemy.ext.asyncio import AsyncSession

     from app.config import settings
     from app.core.capacity import CapacityLimiter
     from app.core.hashing import compute_content_hash
     from app.db import repository as repo
     from app.db.models import ScanStatus
     from app.db.session import get_session
     from app.llm.client import LLMClient, OllamaClient
     from app.reviewer.scanner import run_scan
     from app.schemas import HealthResponse, RuleResultOut, ScanResultResponse, ScanSubmitResponse

     router = APIRouter()

     # Process-wide singletons. A single asyncio.Lock serializes the cache
     # lookup -> create-scan section (see app/db/models.py for the matching
     # defensive partial unique index).
     cache_lock = asyncio.Lock()
     capacity_limiter = CapacityLimiter(max_concurrent=settings.max_parallel_scans)
     llm_client: LLMClient = OllamaClient()
     _background_tasks: set[asyncio.Task] = set()

     def get_capacity_limiter() -> CapacityLimiter:
         return capacity_limiter

     def get_llm_client() -> LLMClient:
         return llm_client

     def get_cache_lock() -> asyncio.Lock:
         return cache_lock

     @router.post("/scans", response_model=ScanSubmitResponse)
     async def submit_scan(
         response: Response,
         file: UploadFile = File(...),
         session: AsyncSession = Depends(get_session),
         limiter: CapacityLimiter = Depends(get_capacity_limiter),
         client: LLMClient = Depends(get_llm_client),
         lock: asyncio.Lock = Depends(get_cache_lock),
     ) -> ScanSubmitResponse:
         if not file.filename or not file.filename.lower().endswith(".py"):
             raise HTTPException(status.HTTP_422_UNPROCESSABLE_CONTENT, "Only .py files are accepted")

         raw = await file.read()
         if not raw:
             raise HTTPException(status.HTTP_422_UNPROCESSABLE_CONTENT, "Uploaded file is empty")
         if len(raw) > settings.max_file_size_bytes:
             raise HTTPException(status.HTTP_422_UNPROCESSABLE_CONTENT, "File exceeds maximum size")

         try:
             code = raw.decode("utf-8")
         except UnicodeDecodeError:
             raise HTTPException(status.HTTP_422_UNPROCESSABLE_CONTENT, "File must be valid UTF-8 text")

         if not code.strip():
             raise HTTPException(status.HTTP_422_UNPROCESSABLE_CONTENT, "Uploaded file is empty")

         content_hash = compute_content_hash(code)

         async with lock:
             await repo.fail_stale_scans(session)
             existing = await repo.find_reusable_scan(session, content_hash)
             if existing is not None:
                 response.status_code = (
                     status.HTTP_200_OK
                     if existing.status == ScanStatus.COMPLETED.value
                     else status.HTTP_202_ACCEPTED
                 )
                 return ScanSubmitResponse(scan_id=existing.id, status=existing.status, cached=True)

             if not limiter.try_acquire():
                 raise HTTPException(
                     status.HTTP_503_SERVICE_UNAVAILABLE,
                     f"Scan capacity reached ({settings.max_parallel_scans} concurrent scans). "
                     "Please try again later.",
                 )

             try:
                 scan = await repo.create_scan(session, content_hash, file.filename, code)
             except Exception:
                 limiter.release()
                 raise

         task = asyncio.create_task(run_scan(scan.id, code, llm_client=client, release_slot=limiter.release))
         _background_tasks.add(task)
         task.add_done_callback(_background_tasks.discard)

         response.status_code = status.HTTP_202_ACCEPTED
         return ScanSubmitResponse(scan_id=scan.id, status=scan.status, cached=False)

     @router.get("/scans/{scan_id}", response_model=ScanResultResponse)
     async def get_scan_result(scan_id: str, session: AsyncSession = Depends(get_session)) -> ScanResultResponse:
         scan = await repo.get_scan(session, scan_id)
         if scan is None:
             raise HTTPException(status.HTTP_404_NOT_FOUND, "Scan not found")

         return ScanResultResponse(
             scan_id=scan.id,
             status=scan.status,
             created_at=scan.created_at,
             results=[
                 RuleResultOut(rule_id=r.rule_id, rule_name=r.rule_name, adheres=r.adheres)
                 for r in scan.results
             ],
         )

     @router.get("/health", response_model=HealthResponse)
     async def health(client: LLMClient = Depends(get_llm_client)) -> HealthResponse:
         return HealthResponse(status="ok", ollama_reachable=await client.is_reachable())
     ```
     Importance: added `await repo.fail_stale_scans(session)` as line 70, inside `cache_lock`, before `find_reusable_scan`. Also `_background_tasks: set[asyncio.Task] = set()` (line 26) and `task.add_done_callback(_background_tasks.discard)` (line 95) are central to the user's most recent (unanswered) question.

   - **`app/main.py`** (MODIFIED) - current full state:
     ```python
     from __future__ import annotations

     import asyncio
     import contextlib
     from collections.abc import AsyncIterator

     from fastapi import FastAPI

     from app.api.routes import router
     from app.config import settings
     from app.db.repository import delete_expired_scans, fail_stale_scans
     from app.db.session import async_session_factory, init_db

     async def _cleanup_loop() -> None:
         while True:
             await asyncio.sleep(settings.cleanup_interval_seconds)
             async with async_session_factory() as session:
                 await fail_stale_scans(session)
                 await delete_expired_scans(session)

     @contextlib.asynccontextmanager
     async def lifespan(app: FastAPI) -> AsyncIterator[None]:
         await init_db()
         task = asyncio.create_task(_cleanup_loop())
         try:
             yield
         finally:
             task.cancel()
             with contextlib.suppress(asyncio.CancelledError):
                 await task

     app = FastAPI(title="Code Review Platform", lifespan=lifespan)
     app.include_router(router)
     ```
     Importance: `task = asyncio.create_task(_cleanup_loop())` at line 26 is the SECOND `create_task` call in the codebase, found via `grep -rn "create_task" app/` which returned exactly:
     ```
     app/main.py:26:    task = asyncio.create_task(_cleanup_loop())
     ```

   - **`app/core/hashing.py`** (READ, unchanged) - full content:
     ```python
     from __future__ import annotations

     import hashlib

     from app.config import settings

     def compute_content_hash(code: str) -> str:
         """Hash of the code plus the rules version and model that will check it.

         Including RULES_VERSION and OLLAMA_MODEL means a cached verdict is only
         reused if it was produced by the same rules and the same model.
         """
         normalized = code.replace("\r\n", "\n").strip()
         payload = f"{settings.rules_version}\0{settings.ollama_model}\0{normalized}"
         return hashlib.sha256(payload.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest()
     ```

   - **`app/db/session.py`** (READ, unchanged) - full content:
     ```python
     from __future__ import annotations

     from collections.abc import AsyncGenerator

     from sqlalchemy import event
     from sqlalchemy.ext.asyncio import AsyncSession, async_sessionmaker, create_async_engine

     from app.config import settings
     from app.db.models import Base

     engine = create_async_engine(settings.database_url)

     @event.listens_for(engine.sync_engine, "connect")
     def _set_sqlite_pragmas(dbapi_connection, _connection_record) -> None:
         cursor = dbapi_connection.cursor()
         cursor.execute("PRAGMA journal_mode=WAL")
         cursor.execute("PRAGMA busy_timeout=5000")
         cursor.execute("PRAGMA foreign_keys=ON")
         cursor.close()

     async_session_factory = async_sessionmaker(engine, expire_on_commit=False)

     async def init_db() -> None:
         async with engine.begin() as conn:
             await conn.run_sync(Base.metadata.create_all)

     async def get_session() -> AsyncGenerator[AsyncSession, None]:
         async with async_session_factory() as session:
             yield session
     ```

I have NOT yet written the explanatory response. I have all context needed (full content of `app/main.py` and `app/api/routes.py` already read/edited this session) to answer both parts:
   - Part 1: Yes, `app/main.py:26` (`_cleanup_loop` task in `lifespan`) is the other background task.
   - Part 2: The local `task` variable in `routes.py`'s `submit_scan` goes out of scope almost immediately (the request handler returns its response right after `asyncio.create_task`), leaving the `Task` object referenced only by the event loop's WEAK reference - per Python's documented asyncio gotcha, such tasks can be garbage-collected mid-execution and silently aborted. `_background_tasks` (module-level `set[asyncio.Task]`) holds a STRONG reference for the task's full lifetime; `task.add_done_callback(_background_tasks.discard)` removes it once finished to prevent unbounded growth. By contrast, `main.py`'s `lifespan` `task` variable lives inside an async generator suspended at `yield` for the app's entire lifetime (naturally stays alive, no GC risk), and `lifespan` explicitly manages its lifecycle via `task.cancel()` + `await task` on shutdown - so no separate set is needed there.

ASSISTANT:
Yes — this is a textbook modular monolith. It's a single deployable process (one FastAPI app, one `uvicorn` invocation, one SQLite file), but internally split into modules with clear boundaries and a single direction of dependency: `api/` (HTTP layer) → `reviewer/` (orchestration) → `llm/` + `db/` (infrastructure), with `core/` as shared low-level utilities.

The clearest signal of "modular" rather than just "organized" is the `LLMClient` protocol in `llm/client.py` — `reviewer/scanner.py` depends on that interface, not on `OllamaClient` directly, so swapping the LLM provider (or running fully in-memory for tests, as the test suite does with `MockLLMClient`) means writing one new class with zero changes elsewhere. Similarly, `db/repository.py` is the only module that touches SQLAlchemy directly — everything else goes through it.

The trade-off you're accepting: it's still one process and one database, so you don't get independent scaling/deployment of, say, the LLM-calling part vs. the API part. For a POC at this scale that's the right call — splitting into actual services would add network/deployment complexity with no real benefit yet, but the module boundaries here mean it *could* be split later (e.g., `reviewer` + `llm` becoming a separate worker service) without a rewrite.

USER:
so in theory if i would like to scale it up and use docker how would i do it in the best way? do not do it, just explain to me

ASSISTANT:
## Two different things "scale it up with Docker" can mean

**1. Containerize for portability (single instance)** — easy, no architecture change.
**2. Horizontal scaling (multiple replicas)** — requires changing a few specific pieces, because right now several things live as **in-process state** in a single Python process.

---

## Stage 1: Dockerize as-is (single replica)

This part is mechanical and doesn't touch the architecture:

- **Dockerfile** for the app: `python:3.12-slim`, install `requirements.txt`, copy `app/`, run `uvicorn app.main:app`.
- **docker-compose.yml** with two services:
  - `app` (your FastAPI container)
  - `ollama` (official Ollama image, with a volume for pulled models)
- `OLLAMA_BASE_URL` in `.env` becomes `http://ollama:11434` (Docker DNS resolves the service name) — this is exactly why `LLMClient`/`OllamaClient` being config-driven pays off, zero code changes needed.
- SQLite file goes on a named volume so it survives container restarts.

At this point you have a portable, reproducible "production-like" setup, but it's still **one app process** — same as running `uvicorn` locally. This alone is often "good enough" scaling for a POC/demo.

---

## Stage 2: True horizontal scaling (N app replicas)

If you literally run N copies of the `app` container behind a load balancer, several pieces of current in-memory state stop being correct:

### 1. `CapacityLimiter` (`core/capacity.py`)
It's an in-memory int counter, one per process. With N replicas each capped at `MAX_PARALLEL_SCANS=5`, your *real* global capacity becomes `N × 5`, not 5. To fix: replace the implementation behind the same `try_acquire()`/`release()` interface with shared state — either:
- **DB-backed**: `SELECT COUNT(*) FROM scans WHERE status='running'` inside a transaction (with row locking or `SELECT ... FOR UPDATE` on a dedicated counter row), or
- **Redis-backed**: atomic `INCR`/`DECR` with a max check.

This is the cleanest abstraction to swap — `core/capacity.py` is already isolated behind that interface.

### 2. `cache_lock` (`asyncio.Lock` in `routes.py`)
Per-process, so it only serializes requests *within one replica*. Two replicas could both pass the "no existing scan for this hash" check at the same instant and both call `create_scan`. To fix:
- Make the **DB-level unique constraint** (the partial unique index on `content_hash` for pending/running rows, mentioned in the plan) the real source of truth, and have `create_scan` catch the resulting `IntegrityError` and fall back to "fetch the existing row" — i.e., turn the lock-based dedup into a DB-constraint + retry pattern. A distributed lock (Redis) is an alternative but heavier than needed here.

### 3. SQLite
SQLite + WAL handles one writer process well, but multiple containers writing to the same file (especially over a shared volume across hosts) gets unreliable — file locking on network filesystems is the classic failure mode, and you'll see more `database is locked` errors even on a single host under real concurrency.
- **Fix**: switch to **Postgres** (its own container/managed service). Because `db/repository.py` is the only module touching SQLAlchemy, this is mostly a `DATABASE_URL` change + swapping the driver (`asyncpg`) + dropping the SQLite-specific PRAGMAs in `db/session.py`. The repository functions themselves barely change.

### 4. The cleanup loop (`_cleanup_loop` in `main.py`)
Each replica's `lifespan` spawns its own copy, so N replicas all run `fail_stale_scans` + `delete_expired_scans` every 10 minutes. It's idempotent (UPDATE/DELETE on conditions), so it's harmless — but wasteful. Cleaner: move it to a **separate single "worker" container** (same image, different entrypoint/command) rather than running it inside every API replica.

### 5. Background scan execution (`asyncio.create_task` in `routes.py`)
Right now, whichever replica receives the `POST /scans` is the one that runs the LLM calls in its own event loop. With multiple replicas this still *works* (each replica processes the scans it accepted), but two consequences:
- If that replica is killed mid-scan, the scan is orphaned in `running` — **this is exactly what the stale-running watchdog we just built now handles**, so it's not a new failure mode, just a more frequent one.
- API throughput and LLM-call throughput are coupled to the same replica count, even though they have very different resource profiles (I/O-bound HTTP handling vs. CPU/GPU-bound LLM inference).

For a more "correct" design at scale, you'd decouple these: `POST /scans` just inserts a `pending` row, and a separate pool of **worker containers** picks up pending scans (poll the DB, or use a real queue like Redis/Arq/Celery). Then "API replicas" and "worker replicas" scale independently.

### 6. Ollama itself
If Ollama is GPU-bound, it's usually the actual bottleneck — and you typically **don't** horizontally scale it the same way as the API. You'd run one (or a few) Ollama instances sized to available GPU(s), and all app/worker replicas point at it/them via `OLLAMA_BASE_URL`. So "scale the app to N replicas" doesn't imply "scale Ollama to N replicas."

---

## Summary

| Component | Stage 1 (single container) | Stage 2 (N replicas) |
|---|---|---|
| App + Ollama | docker-compose, two services | same, + load balancer in front of app |
| `LLMClient` / `OllamaClient` | no change (config-driven) | no change |
| SQLite | fine, on a volume | swap to Postgres |
| `CapacityLimiter` | fine as-is | swap impl to DB/Redis-backed counter |
| `cache_lock` | fine as-is | DB unique constraint + retry instead of in-process lock |
| Cleanup loop | fine as-is | move to a dedicated worker container |
| Scan execution | fine as-is (in-process task) | optionally move to a separate worker pool / queue |

The modular monolith structure keeps Stage 1 trivial and makes most of Stage 2 a matter of **swapping implementations behind existing interfaces** (`LLMClient`, `CapacityLimiter`, `db/repository.py`) rather than a rewrite — except the cache-lock dedup logic, which is the one piece not currently behind a clean seam and would need the most rework.

USER:
let me be more specific - by scaling i mean using a quque (withoutl limitation the numbers of jobs)

ASSISTANT:
## The core shift: "reject at the door" → "accept everything, queue the work"

Right now `POST /scans` does capacity check **before** accepting:
```
no slot free → 503
```
With a queue, every valid upload is accepted and persisted as `pending`, full stop — no 503 from capacity ever. The "max concurrent" concept doesn't disappear, it just **moves from the API to the worker pool** that consumes the queue.

```
POST /scans (validated, cache-checked) → INSERT scans(status='pending') → enqueue(scan_id) → 202
                                                                              |
                                                          [queue, unbounded depth]
                                                                              |
                                                  worker(s) pull jobs, N at a time → run_scan(scan_id, code)
```

---

## What stays the same

- `app/reviewer/scanner.py` (`run_scan`) barely changes — it's already a self-contained async function: `mark_running → per-rule LLM call → parse → save → mark_completed/failed`. It doesn't care *who* calls it.
- `GET /scans/{id}` — unchanged. `pending` now just means "waiting in queue or about to start" instead of "about to be picked up by `asyncio.create_task` in milliseconds."
- The cache/dedup check (`find_reusable_scan`, content hash) — unchanged logic, just no longer gated by a capacity check.
- The stale-running watchdog we just built becomes *more* valuable — a worker crashing mid-job is now the main "stuck `running`" failure mode, and your `fail_stale_scans`/lazy-expiry already covers it.

## What changes

- **`core/capacity.py` (`CapacityLimiter`) goes away from the API entirely.** Concurrency control (your old "5") becomes a **worker-side setting** — e.g., a worker process pulls and runs N jobs concurrently via a semaphore. You can tune this independently of how many requests you accept.
- **`app/api/routes.py`**: `submit_scan` drops the `try_acquire`/503 branch and the `asyncio.create_task(run_scan(...))` + `_background_tasks` bookkeeping. Instead it does `enqueue(scan.id)` and returns 202. The API process no longer runs any scans itself.
- **New component: a worker process** — a small standalone script/container that connects to the queue, pulls job IDs, loads the code from the DB, and calls `run_scan`. This is the piece you'd scale horizontally (more worker replicas = more parallel scans), independent of API replica count — directly answers your earlier Docker question: API and workers become separate services that scale independently.

## Queue technology options (given your stack)

1. **Redis + Arq** — Arq is async-native (same author as pydantic/FastAPI ecosystem), jobs are just `async def` functions, built-in retries and result tracking. Cleanest fit for an all-async codebase, but adds a new piece of infra (Redis).
2. **Postgres `SELECT ... FOR UPDATE SKIP LOCKED`** — no new infrastructure. Workers poll:
   ```sql
   SELECT id FROM scans WHERE status='pending' ORDER BY created_at LIMIT :n FOR UPDATE SKIP LOCKED
   ```
   This is a well-established reliable queue pattern (used by tools like Procrastinate). Since multi-replica scaling already pushes you toward Postgres, this avoids adding Redis purely for queueing.
3. **Celery/RQ** — heavier, more mature ecosystem, but Celery in particular is sync-first and feels like a poor match for an all-async app.

For this project, I'd lean toward **option 2** — it piggybacks on infrastructure you'd already need for multi-replica scaling, and keeps the dependency list small.

## The catch with "unlimited"

Truly unbounded queue depth interacts with your **24h TTL**: if submission rate exceeds processing rate for a sustained period, jobs could sit in `pending` for hours, and a job that finally completes near/after the 24h mark gets deleted almost immediately (or the row expires while still queued, and your lazy-expiry would treat it as "not found" before it's ever processed). In practice "unlimited" usually means "no *hard* per-request rejection," but you'd still want:
- Monitoring on queue depth / oldest-pending-age, and
- Possibly a soft cap that's much higher than 5 (e.g., reject only if queue depth > 10,000) just to protect against runaway disk/memory growth — rather than a hard concurrency cap.

Also worth noting: 202 now means "accepted into queue," not "started" — under heavy load, clients could see `pending` for a long time before it flips to `running`, which is a UX/expectations change worth documenting even though the API contract itself doesn't need a new status value.

USER:
question about the capacity limiter - dont we need to use the lock also when aquiring and realesing capacity?

  [TOOL CALL: Read]
  /Users/idansahar/Desktop/Code Reviewer/app/core/capacity.py

  [TOOL RESULT]
1	from __future__ import annotations
2	
3	
4	class CapacityLimiter:
5	    """Non-blocking concurrency cap.
6	
7	    try_acquire() either reserves a slot and returns True, or returns False
8	    immediately if at capacity. Callers must reject the request on False, not
9	    wait for a slot to free up. Safe in a single-threaded asyncio event loop:
10	    there is no `await` between the capacity check and the increment.
11	    """
12	
13	    def __init__(self, max_concurrent: int) -> None:
14	        if max_concurrent < 1:
15	            raise ValueError("max_concurrent must be at least 1")
16	        self._max_concurrent = max_concurrent
17	        self._in_use = 0
18	
19	    def try_acquire(self) -> bool:
20	        if self._in_use >= self._max_concurrent:
21	            return False
22	        self._in_use += 1
23	        return True
24	
25	    def release(self) -> None:
26	        if self._in_use <= 0:
27	            raise RuntimeError("release() called without a matching try_acquire()")
28	        self._in_use -= 1
29	
30	    @property
31	    def in_use(self) -> int:
32	        return self._in_use
33	

ASSISTANT:
No — `try_acquire()`/`release()` don't need `cache_lock` (or any lock), and the docstring in `core/capacity.py:9-10` says exactly why:

> *"Safe in a single-threaded asyncio event loop: there is no `await` between the capacity check and the increment."*

```python
def try_acquire(self) -> bool:
    if self._in_use >= self._max_concurrent:   # check
        return False
    self._in_use += 1                          # increment
    return True
```

**Why no lock is needed:** asyncio's event loop only switches to another coroutine at an `await` point. `try_acquire()` is a plain synchronous method — once a coroutine starts executing it, it runs to completion (`check` → `increment` → `return`) with zero opportunity for another coroutine to interleave. So even if 100 requests "concurrently" call `try_acquire()`, each call is atomic by construction — there's no window where two callers can both see `_in_use < max` and both increment, overshooting the cap. Same logic applies to `release()`.

**What `cache_lock` is actually for** is a different kind of race — the cache-lookup + create-scan sequence in `routes.py:69-91` **does** contain `await`s:
```python
async with lock:
    await repo.fail_stale_scans(session)          # await — yield point
    existing = await repo.find_reusable_scan(...)  # await — yield point
    if existing is not None:
        ...
    if not limiter.try_acquire():   # <- this line itself needs no lock
        raise HTTPException(...)
    scan = await repo.create_scan(...)             # await — yield point
```
Without `cache_lock`, two concurrent identical-content requests could both execute `find_reusable_scan` (find nothing), both pass `try_acquire`, and both call `create_scan` — duplicate scan rows for the same content, defeating dedup. The lock is there to make *that whole sequence* atomic with respect to other requests.

`try_acquire()` happens to sit inside that locked block, but only because of **sequencing** — it has to run after "cache miss confirmed" and before "create the row" (so a failed `create_scan` can `release()` cleanly via the `except`). It's not there because `try_acquire` itself needs protection; it would be just as correct (and atomic) called outside the lock.

**One caveat, tying back to the Docker/scaling discussion:** this atomicity argument relies entirely on `CapacityLimiter` being an in-memory counter with synchronous methods. If you ever swap it for the DB-backed or Redis-backed counter we discussed for multi-replica scaling, `try_acquire`/`release` *would* contain `await`s (a network round-trip) — at that point you'd need a transaction, row lock, or atomic `INCR`/`DECR` to preserve the same guarantee. So the "no lock needed" property is a direct consequence of the current single-process, in-memory design — not something that survives that particular scaling change for free.

================================================================================
USER:
why not use the asyncio semaphore?

ASSISTANT:
`asyncio.Semaphore` is fundamentally the wrong shape for this requirement, for two related reasons:

## 1. `Semaphore.acquire()` is a *waiting* primitive — there's no non-blocking "try"

```python
async def acquire(self):
    while self._value <= 0:
        fut = self._get_loop().create_future()
        self._waiters.append(fut)
        await fut          # <-- suspends the request handler until someone release()s
    self._value -= 1
    return True
```

Its entire purpose is "block the caller until a slot frees up." But the requirement here is the **opposite**: "if no slot is free, fail *immediately* with 503 — never wait, never queue." `asyncio.Semaphore` doesn't expose a `try_acquire`/non-blocking variant at all (unlike, say, `threading.Lock.acquire(blocking=False)`).

## 2. The "fake it" pattern is the `.locked()` hack the plan explicitly avoided

You *could* write:
```python
if semaphore.locked():        # value == 0
    raise HTTPException(503)
await semaphore.acquire()
```
In CPython's current implementation this happens to be safe — if `_value > 0`, `acquire()` decrements and returns without ever hitting an `await fut`, so it doesn't actually suspend, so no other coroutine can sneak in between the check and the acquire. **But**:

- This relies on an *implementation detail* of `asyncio.Semaphore.acquire()` (that it's synchronous on the fast path), not on anything documented or guaranteed by the API contract. `Semaphore` is documented as a queueing primitive — "doesn't yield if available" isn't a promise it makes.
- It mixes two opposite philosophies on one object (block-and-wait vs. fail-fast), which is confusing for whoever reads it next — they have to reason about asyncio internals to convince themselves it's race-free.

Compare to `CapacityLimiter.try_acquire()` (`core/capacity.py:19-23`) — its correctness is auditable in two lines, with an explicit, intentional contract (`-> bool`, no `await`), and the docstring states *why* it's safe rather than relying on a reader to know asyncio internals.

## 3. `Semaphore.release()` has no leak protection

`Semaphore.release()` just does `self._value += 1` — call it one extra time (e.g., a bug where `release_slot()` runs twice for one scan) and the cap silently becomes "6 concurrent" forever, with no error. `CapacityLimiter.release()` (`core/capacity.py:25-28`) raises `RuntimeError` if `_in_use <= 0`, so a slot-accounting bug fails loudly in tests instead of quietly degrading the concurrency guarantee over time.

## 4. Introspection

`CapacityLimiter.in_use` (`core/capacity.py:30-32`) gives a clean, public "how many slots are used right now" — useful for `/health` or debugging. `Semaphore._value` is a private/internal attribute not meant for that.

**Bottom line**: `Semaphore` models "N permits, wait if none available." This app needs "N permits, fail immediately if none available, and loudly detect double-release bugs." Those are different contracts, and a 15-line purpose-built counter expresses the actual contract directly instead of bending a primitive designed for something else.

================================================================================
USER:
but does the semaphore cant reject the job if it is full at the time? not letting it wait for space to free up

ASSISTANT:
Right — that's exactly the gap. Confirming your understanding precisely:

## Plain `asyncio.Semaphore.acquire()`: if full, it **suspends**, it does not reject

```python
sem = asyncio.Semaphore(5)
# ... 5 slots already taken ...
await sem.acquire()   # <-- the request handler PAUSES here
                       #     and stays paused until some other
                       #     coroutine calls sem.release()
```

There's no exception, no `False` return, nothing — the calling coroutine (your `submit_scan` request handler) just sits suspended in the event loop, waiting for a slot. From the client's perspective, the HTTP request would hang indefinitely (or until timeout) instead of getting an immediate 503. This is the "queueing" behavior — every request eventually gets served, just possibly after a long wait.

## `asyncio.Semaphore` has no non-blocking "try" variant at all

This is the key API gap. Compare to `threading.Semaphore`:
```python
threading.Semaphore.acquire(blocking=False)  # returns True/False immediately — exists!
```
`asyncio.Semaphore.acquire()` has **no `blocking` parameter, and no `acquire_nowait()`**. The only thing close to "check without waiting" is `sem.locked()` (returns `True` if `_value == 0`) — but `locked()` only *reports* state, it doesn't *acquire* anything. To actually get "check-and-acquire-or-fail" behavior you're stuck combining `locked()` + `acquire()`, which is the fragile hack from before — and even that only works because of an implementation detail (that `acquire()` doesn't suspend when a permit is immediately available).

## So, to directly answer: no

`asyncio.Semaphore` literally cannot do "reject immediately if full" as a first-class operation — its one acquisition method is "wait until you can proceed." The requirement here ("6th request → instant 503, never wait") needs a primitive whose **only** operation is "check and immediately return true/false," which is precisely what `CapacityLimiter.try_acquire()` is — a 5-line synchronous function with no `await`, so it can never suspend the caller under any circumstance.

================================================================================
USER:
and this is because we are using a single thread in this system?

ASSISTANT:
Yes, for the `CapacityLimiter` atomicity argument specifically — but it's worth separating that from the `Semaphore` behavior, which is a different kind of "yes."

## `CapacityLimiter`: yes, directly because of single-threaded + cooperative scheduling

In CPython's asyncio, **only one coroutine executes Python bytecode at any instant**, and control only switches to another coroutine at an `await` point (cooperative scheduling — nobody can interrupt you mid-function). So:

```python
def try_acquire(self) -> bool:
    if self._in_use >= self._max_concurrent:
        return False
    self._in_use += 1
    return True
```

Once a request handler enters this function, it runs `check → increment → return` to completion — there is **no instant** where the event loop could pause it and run a different request's `try_acquire()` in between. That's the whole proof of correctness, and it depends entirely on (a) one thread, and (b) no `await` inside.

**Contrast — if this were `threading` instead of `asyncio`:** even with a single CPU core and the GIL, OS threads can be preempted at *any* bytecode instruction, not just at explicit yield points. `self._in_use += 1` is actually `tmp = self._in_use; tmp = tmp + 1; self._in_use = tmp` at the bytecode level — a thread switch could happen between those steps, causing two threads to both read the old value and both write `old+1`, losing an increment. So in a threaded version, you'd need a `threading.Lock` around this. In asyncio, the equivalent "preemption point" is `await`, and since there isn't one here, no lock is needed.

## `asyncio.Semaphore`'s "wait, don't reject" behavior: not really a single-thread consequence

This part is more of an **API design choice**, not a constraint imposed by single-threading. Evidence: `threading.Semaphore` — which *is* multi-threaded — **does** offer `acquire(blocking=False)` for "try and fail immediately." So multi-threading doesn't force a "wait-only" semaphore, and single-threading doesn't force one either. `asyncio.Semaphore` simply chose not to expose a non-blocking variant; "wait until available" is the only mode it models.

**Summary**: the *reason `CapacityLimiter` needs no lock* is single-threaded + no-`await` = atomic. The *reason `asyncio.Semaphore` can't reject-on-full* is just that its API doesn't offer that mode — a library design gap, not a threading-model inevitability.

================================================================================

## Is "false, false" actually wrong?

Walking through `mixed_violations.py` against the actual rule definitions in `rules.yaml`:

**Rule 1 — "All variables have meaningful names"** (rules.yaml:5-9 explicitly calls out *"single-letter names... like `x`"* as violations):
```python
def apply_discount(a, b):
    """apply the discount on a by b"""
    x = a - (a * b)
    return x
```
`a`, `b`, and `x` are all single-letter names — a textbook violation per the rule's own wording. Since the verdict is computed **once for the whole file** (one prompt per rule, not per function), a single bad function makes the whole-file answer **"not all variables have meaningful names" → `adheres=False`**.

**Rule 2 — "Docstring reflects the actual code logic"**:
```python
def get_even_numbers(numbers: list[int]) -> list[int]:
    """Return only the odd numbers from the list."""
    return [n for n in numbers if n % 2 == 0]
```
The docstring says **"odd"**, the code filters for **`% 2 == 0`** (even) — a direct, unambiguous contradiction. Same logic applies: one violating function → whole-file `adheres=False`.

So **`false, false` is actually the *correct* verdict for this file** — both violations are real and pretty blatant (the file is literally named `mixed_violations.py`). The fact that `calculate_total_price` is perfectly clean doesn't change the answer, because both rules are phrased as "all/every" — one bad apple fails the whole file.

One caveat: we can't currently see *why* the model said false, because `scanner.py` discards the LLM's `reason` field —
```python
adheres = parse_verdict(raw)
await _run(repo.save_rule_result, scan_id, rule.id, rule.name, adheres, None)  # <- reason hardcoded to None
```
So we know the answer is *correct*, but not whether the model caught the *right* violation for the *right* reason vs. got lucky. If you want to sanity-check that, persisting/logging `reason` would be a small, useful addition — let me know if you want that.

## Other models worth trying

Given the task is really two things — (a) spotting bad identifier names, and (b) comparing a docstring's claim against the code's actual behavior (a semantic-comparison/reading-comprehension task more than a "write code" task) — a few Ollama-pullable alternatives to `qwen2.5-coder:7b`, roughly cheapest→most expensive:

| Model | Why try it |
|---|---|
| **`llama3.1:8b`** | Strong general instruction-following and reliable JSON-schema output; good "does A match B" reasoning even though it's not code-specialized. |
| **`phi-4:14b`** | Very strong reasoning-per-parameter (Microsoft), often punches above its size on logic/comparison tasks — good fit for rule 2. |
| **`qwen2.5:14b`** (non-coder) | Same family/JSON reliability as your current model, but the general-purpose 14B variant may reason better about "does this docstring describe this code" than the code-completion-tuned `-coder` variant. |
| **`qwen2.5-coder:14b`** | Same family, bigger — most likely to be *more careful* without changing behavior/prompt format, since you already know `:7b` works with your JSON schema. |
| **`gemma2:9b`** | Another solid general reasoner, good at structured output. |

Trade-off to keep in mind: you saw earlier that `qwen2.5-coder:7b` had a cold-reload/memory-pressure issue under load — anything in the 14B range will be slower per call and use significantly more RAM/VRAM, which matters with `MAX_PARALLEL_SCANS=5` potentially keeping the model "hot" under concurrent load.

Since `OLLAMA_MODEL` is fully config-driven (`.env`), trying any of these is just `ollama pull <model>` + changing one line — no code changes. Want me to pull and try one of these against `mixed_violations.py` to compare?

ASSISTANT:
## What "in-flight" means here

A scan is "in-flight" while it's been accepted but hasn't reached a terminal state yet — i.e. `status` is `pending` or `running`. The dedup mechanism needs to catch **two different situations** with the same content hash:

1. **Already finished** (`completed`) → return the existing result, no LLM work needed.
2. **Still in progress** (`pending`/`running`) → don't start a *second* identical scan; just point the new request at the one already running.

(2) is the "in-flight" case — and it's the trickier one, because it involves a **race condition**, not just a lookup.

## The hash itself

`compute_content_hash(code)` (`app/core/hashing.py`):
```python
payload = f"{settings.rules_version}\0{settings.ollama_model}\0{normalized_code}"
return hashlib.sha256(payload.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest()
```
Same code + same `RULES_VERSION` + same `OLLAMA_MODEL` → same hash → eligible for reuse. Change any of those three, and it's treated as a different scan.

## `find_reusable_scan` covers both cases via `REUSABLE_STATUSES`

```python
REUSABLE_STATUSES = (ScanStatus.PENDING.value, ScanStatus.RUNNING.value, ScanStatus.COMPLETED.value)
```
The lookup in `repository.py:24-38` matches on `content_hash` AND `status in REUSABLE_STATUSES` — so a `pending`/`running` row for the same hash is just as "reusable" as a `completed` one. The route then sets the response code based on which it found:
```python
response.status_code = (
    status.HTTP_200_OK if existing.status == ScanStatus.COMPLETED.value
    else status.HTTP_202_ACCEPTED   # in-flight → still 202, but cached:true
)
```

## Why the in-flight case needs `cache_lock`

Imagine two identical uploads arrive at almost the same instant, **before either has created its row**:

```
Client A: find_reusable_scan(hash) → nothing found (A hasn't inserted yet)
Client B: find_reusable_scan(hash) → nothing found (A hasn't inserted yet either)
Client A: create_scan() → row #1, status=pending
Client B: create_scan() → row #2, status=pending   ← duplicate!
```
Without serialization, both pass the "is there already an in-flight scan?" check at the same time — because at that exact moment, *neither* scan exists yet. Result: two rows, two capacity slots consumed, two identical sets of LLM calls.

`cache_lock` (`async with lock:` in `routes.py:69-91`) closes this window by making "check → create" atomic per request:
```
Client A: [lock] find_reusable_scan → nothing → create_scan (row #1, pending) [unlock]
Client B: [lock] find_reusable_scan → finds row #1 (status=pending, in REUSABLE_STATUSES)
          → return cached:true, scan_id=row#1, status="pending" [unlock]
```
B never calls `create_scan`, never consumes a capacity slot, never triggers an LLM call — it just gets handed A's `scan_id` and can poll the same `GET /scans/{id}`.

## The partial unique index — DB-level backstop

`app/db/models.py:33-38`:
```python
Index(
    "uq_scans_content_hash_inflight",
    "content_hash",
    unique=True,
    sqlite_where=text("status IN ('pending', 'running')"),
)
```
This says: **at the database level**, at most one row with a given `content_hash` may have `status IN ('pending','running')` at a time. It's a *partial* index — once a scan moves to `completed`/`failed`, it no longer counts, so old finished rows for the same hash don't conflict with a fresh resubmission.

This index isn't load-bearing for correctness in the current single-process design — `cache_lock` already prevents the duplicate-insert race in-process. It exists as a **backstop**: if you ever ran multiple app instances against a shared DB (the multi-replica scaling scenario we discussed earlier), the in-process `asyncio.Lock` wouldn't span processes, but this DB constraint still would — a second process's `INSERT` for the same in-flight hash would fail at the DB level rather than silently creating a duplicate. (Note: `create_scan` doesn't currently catch/handle that `IntegrityError` — that'd be the piece to add if you actually went multi-replica, as mentioned in that earlier discussion.)

## End-to-end example

1. **A** submits `foo.py` → hash `H`. Cache miss → `create_scan` → row `#1`, `status=pending` → 202, `cached=false`.
2. Background task starts: `mark_running` → row `#1` becomes `status=running`.
3. **B** submits the *same* `foo.py` while `#1` is still `running` → `find_reusable_scan` finds row `#1` (running ∈ `REUSABLE_STATUSES`) → 202, `cached=true`, same `scan_id` as A.
4. Row `#1` finishes → `status=completed`.
5. **C** submits the same `foo.py` later (within TTL) → finds row `#1` (`completed`) → 200, `cached=true` — instant result, zero LLM calls.

If row `#1` had instead ended as `failed` (excluded from `REUSABLE_STATUSES`, per the comment we just added), step 5 would *not* match it — `find_reusable_scan` would return nothing, and a fresh scan would be created and actually run.

ASSISTANT:
This is your editor's **"format on save"** feature — it's running a Python code formatter (the style is unmistakably **Black**, or the Ruff formatter which mimics Black) every time you save a `.py` file.

**It's not coming from this project** — I checked, and there's no `pyproject.toml`, `ruff.toml`, `.flake8`, or `.vscode/settings.json` here, and `requirements-dev.txt` only has `pytest`/`pytest-asyncio`/`httpx` — no formatter. So this must be a **global/user-level setting in your editor** (e.g. VS Code with the "Black Formatter" or "Ruff" extension installed and `editor.formatOnSave: true`), applying to every Python file you save regardless of project.

## What it's actually doing

It enforces Black's default **88-character line limit**. Any line over that gets auto-wrapped:

- **Long function signatures** → one parameter per line, closing `)` on its own line:
  ```python
  async def create_scan(
      session: AsyncSession, content_hash: str, filename: str, code: str
  ) -> Scan:
  ```
- **Long import statements** → expanded to parens, one import per line, trailing comma:
  ```python
  from fastapi import (
      APIRouter,
      Depends,
      ...
  )
  ```
- **Long `if`/boolean conditions** → wrapped in parens, one clause per line (this is what happened to the stale-scan check in `get_scan`)
- **Long tuples/literals** (e.g. `REUSABLE_STATUSES`) → one element per line

## Is it a problem?

No — it's purely cosmetic, doesn't touch behavior (tests are still 26/26). It's a very common, generally-encouraged practice for consistent style across contributors.

## If you want control over it

- **To stop it**: turn off "Format On Save" in your editor settings (or unset the default Python formatter).
- **To make it reproducible** for anyone who clones the repo (so it's not dependent on each person's editor config): add a `pyproject.toml` with a `[tool.black]` (or `[tool.ruff]`) section pinning the line-length, and add the formatter to `requirements-dev.txt`. I haven't done this — just flagging it as an option if you want the formatting to be part of the project rather than incidental to your editor.

Either way, nothing to fix on my end — the reformatting is happening *after* my edits, on your save, and doesn't conflict with anything.

ASSISTANT:
This is almost certainly an **interpreter mismatch** in your editor, not an actual problem with the project.

I confirmed: `.venv` has Python 3.12.12 and all the third-party packages (`fastapi`, `sqlalchemy`, `pydantic_settings`, `httpx`, `yaml`, `ollama`) import fine from it — and `pytest` (which imports all of `app/`) passes 26/26. So the packages genuinely are installed; your **editor's language server (e.g. VS Code + Pylance) is just looking at a different Python interpreter** that doesn't have them.

## Why "some files" specifically

Pylance flags an import red only when it can't find that package for the *currently selected interpreter*. So:
- Files that only import stdlib (e.g. `app/core/capacity.py`, `app/core/hashing.py` — just `hashlib`) → no red, since stdlib resolves regardless of interpreter.
- Files importing `fastapi`, `sqlalchemy`, `pydantic_settings`, `yaml`, `ollama`, `httpx`, `pytest`, etc. (`app/api/routes.py`, `app/db/*.py`, `app/config.py`, `app/reviewer/rules.py`, `app/llm/client.py`, `tests/*.py`) → red, because the interpreter the editor is using doesn't have these in its `site-packages`.

## Fix

In VS Code: `Cmd+Shift+P` → **"Python: Select Interpreter"** → pick the one at:
```
/Users/idansahar/Desktop/Code Reviewer/.venv/bin/python
```
(should show up labeled `Python 3.12.12 ('.venv')`). Once selected, Pylance re-resolves imports against that environment and the red squiggles should clear for those files.

If it doesn't appear in the list, you can enter the path manually via "Enter interpreter path..." in that same picker.

USER:
in the @app/reviewer/scanner.py where do we aqquire capacity? since we only realse it

ASSISTANT:
`scanner.py` never acquires — by the time `run_scan` starts, the slot has **already been acquired by `routes.py`**, before the background task was even created.

## Where acquisition happens

`app/api/routes.py`, inside `submit_scan`, before spawning the task:
```python
if not limiter.try_acquire():
    raise HTTPException(
        status.HTTP_503_SERVICE_UNAVAILABLE,
        f"Scan capacity reached ({settings.max_parallel_scans} concurrent scans). "
        "Please try again later.",
    )

try:
    scan = await repo.create_scan(session, content_hash, file.filename, code)
except Exception:
    limiter.release()
    raise
```

Then the slot's "ownership" is handed off to `run_scan` via the `release_slot` parameter:
```python
task = asyncio.create_task(
    run_scan(scan.id, code, llm_client=client, release_slot=limiter.release)
)
```
`limiter.release` is just a bound method reference — `scanner.py` receives it as a plain callback and calls it once in `finally`, with no idea (and no need to know) that it corresponds to a `CapacityLimiter`.

## Why the split?

The whole point of `try_acquire()` is to let `POST /scans` **reject synchronously with 503** if the system is at capacity — that decision has to be made *before* the HTTP response is sent. If acquisition happened inside `run_scan` instead, the request handler would already have returned `202 Accepted` to the client before discovering there's no room — too late to say "no."

So the **acquire** has to live in the request-handling path (where rejection is still possible), while the **release** has to live wherever the work actually finishes (which is the background task, since the scan can take minutes). `scanner.py` is purely the "consumer" side of an already-reserved slot — its only responsibility is to give it back, success or failure, which is why `finally: release_slot()` is the only capacity-related line in that file.

This is also exactly why the `try/except: limiter.release(); raise` exists right after `try_acquire()` in `routes.py` — if `create_scan` or `asyncio.create_task` itself fails *before* `run_scan` ever starts, nothing would ever call `release_slot()`, so that path has its own explicit release to avoid a permanent slot leak.

15	async def _cleanup_loop() -> None:
16	    """cleanup loop to inforce the 24 TTL"""
17	    while True:
18	        await asyncio.sleep(settings.cleanup_interval_seconds)
19	        async with async_session_factory() as session:
20	            await fail_stale_scans(session)
21	            await delete_expired_scans(session)
22	
23	
24	@contextlib.asynccontextmanager
25	async def lifespan(app: FastAPI) -> AsyncIterator[None]:
26	    await init_db()
27	    task = asyncio.create_task(_cleanup_loop())
28	    try:
29	        yield
30	    finally:
31	        task.cancel()
32	        with contextlib.suppress(asyncio.CancelledError):
33	            await task

2. Key Technical Concepts:
   - asyncio weak-reference task GC gotcha (`_background_tasks` set + `add_done_callback`)
   - Modular monolith pattern (single deployable, module boundaries via `LLMClient` protocol, `db/repository.py`)

   - Queue-based unlimited-jobs architecture: producer/consumer redesign, Arq/Redis vs Postgres `SELECT...FOR UPDATE SKIP LOCKED`, queue-depth vs TTL interaction
   - `CapacityLimiter` atomicity: synchronous methods with no `await` = atomic in single-threaded event loop, no lock needed
   - `asyncio.Semaphore` limitations: no non-blocking acquire, `acquire()` suspends rather than rejects, no leak protection on `release()`
   - Single-threaded cooperative scheduling vs OS thread preemption (why `+=` needs a lock under `threading` but not under asyncio-without-`await`)
   - In-flight cache/dedup: `compute_content_hash` (SHA-256 of rules_version+model+code), `REUSABLE_STATUSES`, `cache_lock` race prevention, partial unique index `uq_scans_content_hash_inflight` as multi-replica DB-level backstop

   - **`app/core/capacity.py`** (read, full content, unchanged):
     ```python
     class CapacityLimiter:
         def __init__(self, max_concurrent: int) -> None:
             if max_concurrent < 1:
                 raise ValueError("max_concurrent must be at least 1")
             self._max_concurrent = max_concurrent
             self._in_use = 0

         def try_acquire(self) -> bool:
             if self._in_use >= self._max_concurrent:
                 return False
             self._in_use += 1
             return True

         def release(self) -> None:
             if self._in_use <= 0:
                 raise RuntimeError("release() called without a matching try_acquire()")
             self._in_use -= 1

         @property
         def in_use(self) -> int:
             return self._in_use
     ```

   - **`app/db/models.py`** (read, full content, unchanged) - key part: `Scan.__table_args__` includes `uq_scans_content_hash_inflight` partial unique index (`unique=True, sqlite_where=text("status IN ('pending', 'running')")`), `REUSABLE_STATUSES` referenced from repository.py.

     ```python
     async def get_scan(
         session: AsyncSession, scan_id: str, ttl_hours: int | None = None
     ) -> Scan | None:
         stmt = (
             select(Scan)
             .where(Scan.id == scan_id, Scan.created_at >= _cutoff(ttl_hours))
             .options(selectinload(Scan.results))
         )
         result = await session.execute(stmt)
         scan = result.scalar_one_or_none()  # for stict max one row
         # Lazy fixup: check if the worker died mid-scan. Otherwise the row would stay

         if (
             scan is not None
             and scan.status == ScanStatus.RUNNING.value
             and scan.created_at < _stale_cutoff()
         ):
             scan.status = ScanStatus.FAILED.value
             scan.completed_at = utcnow()
             scan.error = "Scan timed out"
             await session.commit()
         return scan
     ```

     ```python
     from __future__ import annotations

     from typing import Protocol

     import ollama

     from app.config import settings

     class LLMClient(Protocol):
         async def complete(self, prompt: str, *, json_schema: dict | None = None) -> str:
             """Send a prompt to the model and return its raw text response."""

         async def is_reachable(self) -> bool:
             """Cheap connectivity check used by /health."""

     class OllamaClient:
         """Thin wrapper around ollama.AsyncClient; base_url and model are injected
         so config.py controls which provider/model is used."""

         def __init__(
             self,
             base_url: str | None = None,
             model: str | None = None,
             timeout_seconds: float | None = None,
         ) -> None:
             self._model = model or settings.ollama_model
             self._client = ollama.AsyncClient(
                 host=base_url or settings.ollama_base_url,
                 timeout=timeout_seconds or settings.llm_timeout_seconds,
             )

         async def complete(self, prompt: str, *, json_schema: dict | None = None) -> str:
             """sends the llm a prompt and recive a completion"""  ← TO FIX
             response = await self._client.generate(
                 model=self._model,
                 prompt=prompt,
                 format=json_schema,
                 stream=False,
                 options={"temperature": 0},  # keeps output deterministic
             )
             return response.response

         async def is_reachable(self) -> bool:
             try:
                 await self._client.list()
                 return True
             except Exception:
                 return False
     ```
     **TO FIX**: Line 35 docstring `"""sends the llm a prompt and recive a completion"""` → fix typo "recive"→"receive" and match the Protocol's style (line 12: `"""Send a prompt to the model and return its raw text response."""`). Suggested replacement from the agent: `"""Send a prompt to the model and return the raw completion text."""`

We want to build an automatic Code Review Platform!
We'll start with a simple POC, that will show the full E2E flow - requesting a scan, analyzing the code, and fetching the results.

The system is comprised of 2 parts:
      • The Code-Reviewer: given a source code file (python), it runs a set of pre-defined singular checks (each defined using a prompt) against the code and identifies to which rules the code adheres to and to which it doesn't.
      • The Platform: gets a scan request from the user (containing the code file) via API, scans it (using the Code-Reviewer), and makes its output accessible for fetching via a dedicated API. 

Rules to be checked:
      1) All variables have meaningful names
      2)  docstring of function reflects the actual code’s logic

Requirements:
      1) Rules might be added overtime and total analysis time might exceed connection limitations, therefore scan requesting and results fetching must be separated (asynchronous).
      2) Scan results should show users the list of all checked rules, and for each whether it is adhered to or not (TRUE/FALSE)

ASSISTANT:
Going through these one at a time against the actual code:

## Real bugs — small, safe to fix now

**1. No null-check after `session.get()` in `mark_running`/`mark_completed`/`mark_failed`** — true, `repository.py` does `scan = await session.get(Scan, scan_id); scan.status = ...` with no guard. In practice this can't fire from TTL deletion alone (TTL is 24h, and a freshly-created row's `created_at` is "now"), but it's a one-line defensive fix per function and worth doing.

**2. PENDING scans never caught by `fail_stale_scans`** — this is the real one. `fail_stale_scans` only matches `status == RUNNING`. If the process restarts while a scan is `PENDING` (the in-memory `asyncio.create_task` is gone), that row stays `PENDING` forever. Since `PENDING` is in `REUSABLE_STATUSES`, every future submission of that content hash will hit `find_reusable_scan`, get this dead row back, and return `cached:true, status:pending` — forever, with nothing actually running. **Fix**: widen `fail_stale_scans`'s filter to `status.in_([PENDING, RUNNING])` and `created_at < _stale_cutoff()`. Same one-line idea for the lazy fixup in `get_scan`. Cheap, single-file, directly closes a real "stuck forever" bug.

**4. Slot leak if `asyncio.create_task()` itself raises** — only realistically happens if `run_scan(...)` raises while constructing the coroutine (signature bug), so it's a defensive-only fix. Still trivial to wrap and release the slot + mark the row failed in one `except`.

## Overstated / not actually a problem

**3. "asyncio.Lock serializes all submissions, defeating 5 parallel scans"** — this conflates two different things. `cache_lock` only wraps `fail_stale_scans` + `find_reusable_scan` + `create_scan` — a few SQLite queries, sub-millisecond. The actual "5 parallel scans" are the 5 concurrent `run_scan()` background tasks doing LLM calls, which run completely **outside** the lock. The lock doesn't touch scan execution at all — it's a textbook short critical section around an atomic check-then-insert. This claim is incorrect as stated.

The *last* bullet (replace the lock with `IntegrityError` on the partial unique index) is a related but separate, more legitimate point — it's about which primitive does the dedup, and matters for multi-process scaling, not for parallelism within this process. Doesn't change scan throughput either way.

## Big architectural points — valid, but bigger calls

**A. No service layer** — fair style critique (business logic lives in `submit_scan`), but it's ~150 lines, not unreasonable for a POC. Extracting to `app/reviewer/service.py` is a moderate refactor across `routes.py` (and tests).

**B. Sequential rules instead of `asyncio.gather`** — valid in principle and "free" to try, but with a single local Ollama instance serving one 7B model, concurrent requests mostly queue server-side rather than overlapping — the latency win may be small in practice. Worth doing, but adds complexity around per-rule timeout/error handling when run concurrently.

**C. `asyncio.create_task` fire-and-forget loses scans on restart; DB-backed PENDING-as-queue** — this is the sharpest point and the *root cause* of #2. The "real" fix is a worker loop that polls for `PENDING` rows instead of spawning tasks directly from the route handler — which is exactly the queue-based redesign we discussed earlier for Docker scaling. That's a genuine architecture change, not a bug fix.

## My recommendation

Fix **#1, #2, #4** now — they're small, single-file, low-risk, and #2 is a legitimate correctness bug. I'd leave **A, B, C, and the lock-vs-IntegrityError point** alone given you're close to submitting and per your global rules I shouldn't make structural changes without checking first — but they're great material for a "Known Limitations / Future Work" section in the README (which directly maps to the assignment's "future-ready" framing and shows you understand the tradeoffs).

Want me to go ahead with #1/#2/#4, and/or add a Future Work section covering A/B/C?
