## Part C: what a DB-backed queue would actually take

**Important scoping point first**: the assignment's "no queueing of 503s" rule stays intact either way — a request that's rejected for being over capacity still gets rejected immediately. What changes is what happens to the *accepted* (202) scans: instead of an in-memory `asyncio.create_task` that vanishes on restart, the `PENDING` row in the DB *is* the queue entry, and a persistent worker pool processes it. Two different things.

### The shape of the redesign

**1. Replace per-request task spawning with a fixed worker pool**
- In `app/main.py`'s `lifespan`, instead of (or alongside) `_cleanup_loop`, spawn `MAX_PARALLEL_SCANS` long-running `worker_loop()` tasks at startup.
- Each worker loops forever: claim the oldest `PENDING` scan → run it → repeat. If nothing is pending, sleep briefly (e.g. 1s) and poll again.

**2. Add an atomic "claim" operation in `repository.py`**
- New function, e.g. `claim_next_pending_scan(session)`, that does an atomic `UPDATE scans SET status='running' WHERE id = (SELECT id FROM scans WHERE status='pending' ORDER BY created_at LIMIT 1) RETURNING *`.
- SQLite is single-writer, so a single `UPDATE...RETURNING` statement is inherently atomic — two workers can never claim the same row, no extra locking needed. (On Postgres you'd use `SELECT ... FOR UPDATE SKIP LOCKED` instead — same idea.)

**3. `submit_scan` gets much thinner**
- Validate file → hash → cache check (unchanged) → insert `PENDING` row → return 202. That's it.
- No `asyncio.create_task`, no `_background_tasks` set, no `release_slot` callback threaded through `run_scan`.

**4. Capacity check moves from memory to the DB**
- `CapacityLimiter` (in-memory int counter) goes away entirely.
- The 503-on-full check becomes `SELECT COUNT(*) FROM scans WHERE status IN ('pending','running')` ≥ `MAX_PARALLEL_SCANS` → 503. This is correct even across multiple processes, since it reads shared DB state — which is exactly what makes this "future-ready" for the multi-replica Docker scenario we discussed earlier.

**5. `scanner.py` simplifies**
- `run_scan` drops the `release_slot` parameter — there's no slot to release; the worker just becomes free to claim the next row.

**6. Restart recovery is now automatic**
- Any row left `PENDING` (or `RUNNING`, from a crash mid-scan) at restart is picked up naturally: `PENDING` rows get claimed by the worker pool as soon as it starts; `RUNNING` rows from before the crash get marked `failed` by `fail_stale_scans` (fix #2 above) after `scan_stale_seconds`, so they don't block reuse forever — though they aren't auto-retried, consistent with "no auto-retry" being out of scope.

**7. Tests need rework**
- `tests/test_capacity.py` currently relies on `CapacityLimiter` and one `asyncio.create_task` per request — with a fixed worker pool, "5 already running" has to be simulated by seeding DB rows and/or running a real worker loop in the test, and the 503 check becomes a DB-count assertion rather than a limiter-state assertion.
- `tests/test_api.py`'s submit→poll flow would need either a running worker loop in the test fixture, or a way to call `run_scan` directly (bypassing the worker) for tests that don't care about queueing.

### Bottom line on scope

This touches `routes.py`, `scanner.py`, `main.py`, `repository.py`, removes `core/capacity.py` entirely, adds a new worker module, and reworks at least 2 test files. It's a real architectural change — not a bug fix — and per your rules I'd want explicit go-ahead before starting it, especially this close to submission. If you want, a good middle ground for the README's "Future Work" section is to describe exactly this design (it demonstrates the understanding without the risk of a pre-submission rewrite).

================================================================================
USER:
so we basicly creates a 5 worker pool? so i can we reject a new work from being proccesed?

ASSISTANT:
Right — a fixed pool of 5 long-running worker coroutines, each capable of running one scan at a time. Max concurrent execution = 5, by construction.

"Reject when full" still happens **at submission time, before anything is queued** — it doesn't move into the worker pool at all:

```
POST /scans:
  cache check (unchanged)
  count = SELECT COUNT(*) FROM scans WHERE status IN ('pending','running')
  if count >= 5:
      return 503   ← rejected outright, nothing inserted, never enters the queue
  insert PENDING row
  return 202
```

So the "queue" (`PENDING` rows) and "reject on full" are two separate mechanisms answering two different questions:

- **Reject check** = "is the system already at 5 concurrent?" → if yes, refuse to accept the work at all.
- **Queue (`PENDING` row)** = a tiny waiting room for work that *was* accepted but hasn't been picked up by a worker yet.

In steady state, that waiting room should be essentially empty — if `count < 5`, there's always at least one idle worker, and it'll claim the new `PENDING` row within milliseconds (one poll cycle). So in practice a request either gets 503 immediately, or gets 202 and is picked up almost instantly — there's no meaningful "waiting in line" experience, which matches requirement #3 (no queueing of accepted-but-delayed work, just instant accept-or-reject).

One subtlety: just like today's `cache_lock`, the **count-check + insert must be serialized** — otherwise two simultaneous requests could both see `count = 4`, both insert, and you'd briefly have 6 in flight. Same lock, same place, just checking a DB count instead of `CapacityLimiter.try_acquire()`.

================================================================================
USER:
and do you think it is necassery fix?

ASSISTANT:
No — I'd call it valuable but not *necessary*. The actual bug (a `PENDING` row stuck forever, silently blocking re-scans) is already closed by fix #2: a lost-on-restart scan now fails within `scan_stale_seconds` (~5 min) and frees up the content hash for a normal resubmission — which matches the "no auto-retry, user just retries" behavior the assignment already expects for 503s.

The full worker-pool redesign mainly buys you "the original scan resumes automatically after a crash," which isn't one of the 6 stated requirements and sits right next to the explicitly out-of-scope items (queueing, distributed processing, performance tuning). Given it's a 5+ file architectural rewrite with test rewrites, and you're close to submitting, I'd write it up in a "Future Work / Known Limitations" section instead — it demonstrates the same depth of understanding without any risk to what's currently working and tested.
